border-bottom: 2px solid #A2070F">Footnotes</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Lords Spiritual sit on the Government benches.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Excludes 23 peers on leave of absence or otherwise disqualified from sitting.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>House of Lords of the United Kingdom</b>, also known as the <b>House of Peers</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Upper_house" title="Upper house">upper house</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>. Like the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>, it meets in the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Officially, the full name of the house is <b>the Right Honourable the Lords Spiritual and Temporal of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland in Parliament assembled</b>.\n</p><p>Unlike the elected House of Commons, all <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Members of the House of Lords">members of the House of Lords</a> (excluding 90 <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_peer" title="Hereditary peer">hereditary peers</a> elected among themselves and two peers who are <i>ex officio</i> members) are appointed.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The membership of the House of Lords is drawn from <a href="/wiki/Peerages_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Peerages in the United Kingdom">the peerage</a> and is made up of <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lords_Temporal" title="Lords Temporal">Lords Temporal</a>. The Lords Spiritual are 26 <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Of the Lords Temporal, the majority are <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peers</a> who are appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">monarch</a> on the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>, or on the advice of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Appointments_Commission" title="House of Lords Appointments Commission">House of Lords Appointments Commission</a>. However, they also include some hereditary peers including four dukes.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Membership was once an entitlement of all hereditary peers, other than those in the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_Ireland" title="Peerage of Ireland">peerage of Ireland</a>, but under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Act_1999" title="House of Lords Act 1999">House of Lords Act 1999</a>, the right to membership was restricted to <a href="/wiki/List_of_elected_hereditary_peers_under_the_House_of_Lords_Act_1999" title="List of elected hereditary peers under the House of Lords Act 1999">92 hereditary peers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Since 2008, only one of them is female (the <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Mar,_31st_Countess_of_Mar" title="Margaret of Mar, 31st Countess of Mar">Countess of Mar</a>); most hereditary peerages can be inherited only by men.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> has a defined 650-seat membership, the number of members in the House of Lords is not fixed. There are currently 791 sitting Lords. The House of Lords is the only upper house of any <a href="/wiki/Bicameral_parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicameral parliament">bicameral parliament</a> to be larger than its lower house.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The House of Lords scrutinises <a href="/wiki/Bill_(law)" title="Bill (law)">bills</a> that have been approved by the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It regularly reviews and amends Bills from the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> While it is unable to prevent Bills passing into law, except in certain limited circumstances,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> it can delay Bills and force the Commons to reconsider their decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In this capacity, the House of Lords acts as a check on the House of Commons that is independent from the electoral process.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Bills can be introduced into either the House of Lords or the House of Commons. While members of the Lords may also take on roles as government ministers, high-ranking officials such as cabinet ministers are usually drawn from the Commons. The House of Lords has its own support services, separate from the Commons, including the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Library" title="House of Lords Library">House of Lords Library</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Speech_from_the_throne" title="Speech from the throne">Queen\'s Speech</a> is delivered in the House of Lords during the State Opening of Parliament. In addition to its role as the upper house, until the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/UK_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Supreme Court">Supreme Court</a> in 2009, the House of Lords, through the <a href="/wiki/Law_Lords" class="mw-redirect" title="Law Lords">Law Lords</a>, acted as the final court of appeal in the United Kingdom judicial system.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The House also has a Church of England role, in that <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England_Assembly_(Powers)_Act_1919" title="Church of England Assembly (Powers) Act 1919">Church Measures</a> must be tabled within the House by the Lords Spiritual.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" counterparts</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(October 2009)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Today\'s Parliament of the United Kingdom largely descends, in practice, from the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>, though the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> of 1706 and the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union,_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts of Union, 1707">Acts of Union</a> that ratified the Treaty in 1707 created a new <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a> to replace the Parliament of England and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a>. This new parliament was, in effect, the continuation of the Parliament of England with the addition of 45 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MPs</a> and 16 Peers to represent Scotland.\n</p><p>The House of Lords developed from the "Great Council" (<i><a href="/wiki/Magnum_Concilium" title="Magnum Concilium">Magnum Concilium</a></i>) that advised the King during medieval times.<sup id="cite_ref-loveland1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loveland1-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This royal council came to be composed of ecclesiastics, noblemen, and representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England" title="Historic counties of England">counties of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_Wales" title="Historic counties of Wales">Wales</a> (afterwards, representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_borough" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary borough">boroughs</a> as well). The first English Parliament is often considered to be the "<a href="/wiki/Model_Parliament" title="Model Parliament">Model Parliament</a>" (held in 1295), which included archbishops, bishops, abbots, earls, barons, and representatives of the shires and boroughs of it.\n</p><p>The power of Parliament grew slowly, fluctuating as the strength of the monarchy grew or declined. For example, during much of the reign of <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> (1307\xe2\x80\x931327), the <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> was supreme, the <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">Crown</a> weak, and the shire and borough representatives entirely powerless. In 1569, the authority of Parliament was for the first time recognised not simply by custom or royal charter, but by an authoritative statute, passed by Parliament itself.\n</p><p>During the reign of Edward II\'s successor, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, Parliament clearly separated into two distinct <a href="/wiki/Debating_chamber" class="mw-redirect" title="Debating chamber">chambers</a>: the House of Commons (consisting of the shire and borough representatives) and the House of Lords (consisting of the bishops, abbots and <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_England" title="Peerage of England">peers</a>). The authority of Parliament continued to grow, and during the early 15th century both Houses exercised powers to an extent not seen before. The Lords were far more powerful than the Commons because of the great influence of the great landowners and the prelates of the realm.\n</p><p>The power of the nobility declined during the civil wars of the late 15th century, known as the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>. Much of the nobility was killed on the battlefield or executed for participation in the war, and many aristocratic estates were lost to the Crown. Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> was dying, and the <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> armies controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Barons" class="mw-redirect" title="Barons">barons</a> became obsolete. Henry VII (1485\xe2\x80\x931509) clearly established the supremacy of the monarch, symbolised by the "Crown Imperial". The domination of the Sovereign continued to grow during the reigns of the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor monarchs</a> in the 16th century. The Crown was at the height of its power during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> (1509\xe2\x80\x931547).\n</p><p>The House of Lords remained more powerful than the House of Commons, but the Lower House continued to grow in influence, reaching a zenith in relation to the House of Lords during the middle 17th century. Conflicts between the King and the Parliament (for the most part, the House of Commons) ultimately led to the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> during the 1640s. In 1649, after the defeat and execution of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> was declared, but the nation was effectively under the overall control of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector of England, Scotland and Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-abolishact_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abolishact-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The House of Lords was reduced to a largely powerless body, with Cromwell and his supporters in the Commons dominating the Government. On 19 March 1649, the House of Lords was abolished by an Act of Parliament, which declared that "The Commons of England [find] by too long experience that the House of Lords is useless and dangerous to the people of England."<sup id="cite_ref-abolishact_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abolishact-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The House of Lords did not assemble again until the <a href="/wiki/Convention_Parliament_(1660)" title="Convention Parliament (1660)">Convention Parliament</a> met in 1660 and the monarchy was restored. It returned to its former position as the more powerful chamber of Parliament\xe2\x80\x94a position it would occupy until the 19th century.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg/220px-Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg/330px-Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg/440px-Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1292" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Anne_in_the_House_of_Lords_by_Peter_Tillemans.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> addressing the House of Lords, c. 1708\xe2\x80\x9314, by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tillemans" title="Peter Tillemans">Peter Tillemans</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_lords_and_princes_chamber.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A monochrome illustration of several short buildings clustered in a small space. wall of the House of Lords in the centre.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg" class="image"><img 1909.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" 2x" data-file-width="1554" data-file-height="932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The House of Lords voting for the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 19th century was marked by several changes to the House of Lords. The House, once a body of only about 50&#160;members, had been greatly enlarged by the liberality of <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George&#160;III</a> and his successors in creating peerages. The individual influence of a Lord of Parliament was thus diminished.\n</p><p>Moreover, the power of the House as a whole decreased, whilst that of the House of Commons grew. Particularly notable in the development of the Lower House\'s superiority was the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Bill of 1832</a>. The electoral system of the House of Commons was far from democratic: property qualifications greatly restricted the size of the electorate, and the boundaries of many constituencies had not been changed for centuries.\n</p><p>Entire cities such as <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> were not represented by a single individual in the House of Commons, but the 11 voters of <a href="/wiki/Old_Sarum_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Old Sarum (UK Parliament constituency)">Old Sarum</a> retained their ancient right to elect two MPs. A small borough was susceptible to bribery, and was often under the control of a patron, whose nominee was guaranteed to win an election. Some aristocrats were patrons of numerous "<a href="/wiki/Rotten_borough" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotten borough">pocket boroughs</a>", and therefore controlled a considerable part of the membership of the House of Commons.\n</p><p>When the House of Commons passed a Reform Bill to correct some of these anomalies in 1831, the House of Lords rejected the proposal. The popular cause of reform, however, was not abandoned by the ministry, despite a second rejection of the bill in 1832. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey</a> advised the King to overwhelm opposition to the bill in the House of Lords by creating about 80&#160;new pro-Reform peers. <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> originally balked at the proposal, which effectively threatened the opposition of the House of Lords, but at length relented.\n</p><p>Before the new peers were created, however, the Lords who opposed the bill admitted defeat and abstained from the vote, allowing the passage of the bill. The crisis damaged the political influence of the House of Lords but did not altogether end it. A vital reform was effected by the Lords themselves in 1868, when they changed their standing orders to abolish proxy voting, preventing Lords from voting without taking the trouble to attend.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Over the course of the century the power of the Upper House were further eroded,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Please clarify the following statement or statements with a good explanation from a reliable source. (July 2013)">how?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the Commons gradually became the stronger House of Parliament.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg/300px-1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" width="300" height="417" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/41/1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1911-New-Perrs-UK-Punch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Punch</i> 1911 cartoon shows Asquith and Lloyd George preparing coronets for 500 new peers to threaten takeover of House of Lords</div></div></div>\n<p>The status of the House of Lords returned to the forefront of debate after the election of a Liberal Government in 1906. In 1909, the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, David Lloyd George, introduced into the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> the "People\'s Budget", which proposed a land tax targeting wealthy landowners. The popular measure, however, was defeated in the heavily Conservative House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Having made the powers of the House of Lords a primary campaign issue, the Liberals were narrowly re-elected in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_January_1910" title="United Kingdom general election, January 1910">January 1910</a>. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> then proposed that the powers of the House of Lords be severely curtailed. After a further general election in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_December_1910" title="United Kingdom general election, December 1910">December 1910</a>, and with an undertaking by King <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a> to create sufficient new Liberal peers to overcome Lords\' opposition to the measure if necessary, the Asquith Government secured the passage of a bill to curtail the powers of the House of Lords.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a> effectively abolished the power of the House of Lords to reject legislation, or to amend it in a way unacceptable to the House of Commons: most bills could be delayed for no more than three parliamentary sessions or two calendar years. It was not meant to be a permanent solution; more comprehensive reforms were planned. Neither party, however, pursued the matter with much enthusiasm, and the House of Lords remained primarily hereditary. In 1949, the Parliament Act reduced the delaying power of the House of Lords further to two sessions or one year.\n</p><p>In 1958, the predominantly hereditary nature of the House of Lords was changed by the <a href="/wiki/Life_Peerages_Act_1958" title="Life Peerages Act 1958">Life Peerages Act 1958</a>, which authorised the creation of life baronies, with no numerical limits. The number of Life Peers then gradually increased, though not at a constant rate.\n</p><p>The Labour Party had for most of the 20th century a commitment, based on the party\'s historic opposition to class privilege, to abolish the House of Lords, or at least expel the hereditary element. In 1968, the Labour Government of <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> attempted to reform the House of Lords by introducing a system under which hereditary peers would be allowed to remain in the House and take part in debate, but would be unable to vote. This plan, however, was defeated in the House of Commons by a coalition of traditionalist Conservatives (such as <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a>), and Labour members who continued to advocate the outright abolition of the Upper House (such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>).\n</p><p>When Michael Foot became leader of the Labour Party in 1980, abolition of the House of Lords became a part of the party\'s agenda; under his successor, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, however, a reformed Upper House was proposed instead. In the meantime, the creation of hereditary peerages (except for members of the Royal Family) has been arrested, with the exception of three creations during the administration of the Conservative <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> in the 1980s.\n</p><p>Whilst some hereditary peers were at best apathetic, the Labour Party\'s clear commitments were not lost on <a href="/wiki/Merlin_Hanbury-Tracy,_7th_Baron_Sudeley" title="Merlin Hanbury-Tracy, 7th Baron Sudeley">Merlin Hanbury-Tracy, 7th Baron Sudeley</a>, who for decades was considered an expert on the House of Lords. In December 1979 the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Monday_Club" title="Conservative Monday Club">Conservative Monday Club</a> published his extensive paper entitled <i>Lords Reform \xe2\x80\x93 Why tamper with the House of Lords?</i> and in July 1980 <a href="/wiki/International_Monarchist_League" title="International Monarchist League"><i>The Monarchist</i></a> carried another article by Sudeley entitled <i>Why Reform or Abolish the House of Lords?</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In 1990 he wrote a further booklet for the Monday Club entitled <i>The Preservation of the House of Lords</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lords_reform">Lords reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lords reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reform_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Reform of the House of Lords">Reform of the House of Lords</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_admission_of_women">First admission of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First admission of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There were no women sitting in the House of Lords until 1958, when a small number came into the chamber as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Life_Peerages_Act_1958" title="Life Peerages Act 1958">Life Peerages Act 1958</a>. One of these was <a href="/wiki/Irene_Curzon,_2nd_Baroness_Ravensdale" title="Irene Curzon, 2nd Baroness Ravensdale">Irene Curzon, 2nd Baroness Ravensdale</a>, who had inherited her father\'s peerage in 1925 and was made a life peer to enable her to sit. After a campaign stretching back in some cases to the 1920s, another twelve women who held hereditary peerages in their own right were finally admitted by the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_Act_1963" title="Peerage Act 1963">Peerage Act 1963</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1997.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1997\xe2\x80\x932010">1997\xe2\x80\x932010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1997\xe2\x80\x932010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Labour Party included in its 1997 general election <a href="/wiki/Manifesto" title="Manifesto">Manifesto</a> a commitment to remove the hereditary peerage from the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Their subsequent election victory in 1997 under <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> led to the denouement of the traditional House of Lords. The Labour Government introduced legislation to expel all hereditary peers from the Upper House as a first step in Lords reform. As a part of a compromise, however, it agreed to permit 92 hereditary peers to remain until the reforms were complete. Thus all but 92 hereditary peers were expelled under the House of Lords Act 1999 (see below for its provisions), making the House of Lords predominantly an appointed house.\n</p><p>Since 1999, however, no further reform has taken place. The <a href="/wiki/Wakeham_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Wakeham Commission">Wakeham Commission</a> proposed introducing a 20% elected element to the Lords, but this plan was widely criticised.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Joint_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint Committee">Joint Committee</a> was established in 2001 to resolve the issue, but it reached no conclusion and instead gave Parliament seven options to choose from (fully appointed, 20% elected, 40% elected, 50% elected, 60% elected, 80%, and fully elected). In a confusing series of votes in February 2003, all of these options were defeated, although the 80% elected option fell by just three votes in the Commons. Socialist MPs favouring outright abolition voted against all the options.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, a cross-party group of senior MPs (<a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tyler" title="Paul Tyler">Paul Tyler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Wright_(Staffordshire_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tony Wright (Staffordshire politician)">Tony Wright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_George_Young,_6th_Baronet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir George Young, 6th Baronet">George Young</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Cook" title="Robin Cook">Robin Cook</a>) published a report proposing that 70% of members of the House of Lords should be elected \xe2\x80\x94 each member for a single long term \xe2\x80\x94 by the <a href="/wiki/Single_transferable_vote" title="Single transferable vote">single transferable vote</a> system. Most of the remainder were to be appointed by a Commission to ensure a mix of "skills, knowledge and experience". This proposal was also not implemented. A cross-party campaign initiative called "<a href="/wiki/Elect_the_Lords" class="mw-redirect" title="Elect the Lords">Elect the Lords</a>" was set up to make the case for a predominantly elected Second Chamber in the run up to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>.\n</p><p>At the 2005 election, the Labour Party proposed further reform of the Lords, but without specific details.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative Party, which had, prior to 1997, opposed any tampering with the House of Lords<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, favoured an 80% elected Second Chamber, while the Liberal Democrats called for a fully elected <a href="/wiki/Senate" title="Senate">Senate</a>. During 2006, a cross-party committee discussed Lords reform, with the aim of reaching a consensus: its <a href="/wiki/Reform_of_the_House_of_Lords#2007_white_paper" title="Reform of the House of Lords">findings</a> were published in early 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 7 March 2007, members of the House of Commons voted ten times on a variety of alternative compositions for the upper chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Outright abolition, a wholly appointed house, a 20% elected house, a 40% elected house, a 50% elected house and a 60% elected house were all defeated in turn. Finally the vote for an 80% elected chamber was won by 305 votes to 267, and the vote for a wholly elected chamber was won by an even greater margin: 337 to 224. Significantly this last vote represented an overall majority of MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Furthermore, examination of the names of MPs voting at each division shows that, of the 305 who voted for the 80% elected option, 211 went on to vote for the 100% elected option. Given that this vote took place after the vote on 80% \xe2\x80\x93 whose result was already known when the vote on 100% took place \xe2\x80\x93 this showed a clear preference for a fully elected upper house among those who voted for the only other option that passed. But this was nevertheless only an indicative vote and many political and legislative hurdles remained to be overcome for supporters of an elected second chamber. The House of Lords, soon after, rejected this proposal and voted for an entirely appointed House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2008, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Justice" title="Secretary of State for Justice">Secretary of State for Justice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, introduced a <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a> to the House of Commons proposing to replace the House of Lords with an 80\xe2\x80\x93100% elected chamber, with one third being elected at each general election, for a term of approximately 12\xe2\x80\x9315 years.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The white paper states that as the peerage would be totally separated from membership of the upper house, the name "House of Lords" would no longer be appropriate: It goes on to explain that there is cross-party consensus for the new chamber to be titled the "Senate of the United Kingdom"; however, to ensure the debate remains on the role of the upper house rather than its title, the white paper is neutral on the title of the new house.\n</p><p>On 30 November 2009, a <i>Code of Conduct for Members of the House of Lords</i> was agreed by them; certain amendments were agreed by them on 30 March 2010 and on 12 June 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_parliamentary_expenses_scandal" title="United Kingdom parliamentary expenses scandal">scandal over expenses</a> in the Commons was at its highest pitch only six months before, and the Labourite leadership under <a href="/wiki/Janet_Royall,_Baroness_Royall_of_Blaisdon" title="Janet Royall, Baroness Royall of Blaisdon">Janet Royall</a> determined that something sympathetic should be done.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In Meg Russell\'s article "Is the House of Lords already reformed?", she states three essential features of a legitimate House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-russell_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russell-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The first is that it must have adequate powers over legislation to make the government think twice before making a decision. The House of Lords, she argues, currently has enough power to make it relevant. During Tony Blair\xe2\x80\x99s first year, he was defeated 38 times in the Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-treadwell_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treadwell-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Secondly, as to the composition of the Lords, Meg Russell suggests that the composition must be distinct from the Commons, otherwise it would render the Lords useless. The third feature is the perceived legitimacy of the Lords. She writes, "In general legitimacy comes with election."<sup id="cite_ref-russell_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russell-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-treadwell_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treadwell-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>If the Lords have a distinct and elected composition, this would probably come about through fixed term proportional representation. If this happens, then the perceived legitimacy of the Lords could arguably outweigh the legitimacy of the Commons. This would especially be the case if the House of Lords had been elected more recently than the House of Commons as it could be said to reflect the will of the people better than the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-treadwell_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treadwell-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this scenario, there may well come a time when the Lords twice reject a Bill from the Commons and it is forced through. This would in turn trigger questions about the amount of power the Lords should have and there would be pressure for it to increase. This hypothetical process is known as the "circumnavigation of power theory".<sup id="cite_ref-treadwell_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treadwell-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It implies that it would never be in any government\'s interest to legitimise the Lords, as they would be forfeiting their own power.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="2010.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010\xe2\x80\x93present">2010\xe2\x80\x93present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2010\xe2\x80\x93present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Conservative\xe2\x80\x93Liberal Democrat coalition agreed, after the 2010 general election, to outline clearly a provision for a wholly or mainly elected second chamber, elected by proportional representation. These proposals sparked a debate on 29 June 2010. As an interim measure, appointment of new peers would reflect the shares of the vote secured by the political parties in the last general election.\n</p><p>Detailed proposals for Lords reform, including a draft House of Lords Reform Bill, were published on 17 May 2011. These included a 300-member hybrid house, of whom 80% would be elected. A further 20% would be appointed, and reserve space would be included for some Church of England bishops. Under the proposals, members would also serve single non-renewable terms of 15&#160;years. Former MPs would be allowed to stand for election to the Upper House, but members of the Upper House would not be immediately allowed to become MPs.\n</p><p>The details of the proposal were:<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>The upper chamber shall continue to be known as the House of Lords for legislative purposes.</li>\n<li>The reformed House of Lords should have 300 members of whom 240 are "Elected Members" and 60 appointed "Independent Members". Up to 12 Church of England bishops may sit in the house as <i>ex officio</i> "Lords Spiritual".</li>\n<li>Elected Members will serve a single, non-renewable term of 15&#160;years.</li>\n<li>Elections to the reformed Lords should take place at the same time as elections to the House of Commons.</li>\n<li>Elected Members should be elected using the <a href="/wiki/Single_Transferable_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Single Transferable Vote">Single Transferable Vote</a> system of proportional representation.</li>\n<li>Twenty Independent Members (a third) shall take their seats within the reformed house at the same time as elected members do so, and for the same 15-year term.</li>\n<li>Independent Members will be appointed by the Queen after being proposed by the Prime Minister acting on advice of an Appointments Commission.</li>\n<li>There will no longer be a link between the peerage system and membership of the upper house.</li>\n<li>The current powers of the House of Lords would not change and the House of Commons shall retain its status as the primary House of Parliament.</li></ul>\n<p>The proposals were considered by a Joint Committee on House of Lords Reform made up of both MPs and Peers, which issued its final report on 23 April 2012, making the following suggestions:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>The reformed House of Lords should have 450 members.</li>\n<li>Party groupings, including the Crossbenchers, should choose which of their members are retained in the transition period, with the percentage of members allotted to each group based on their share of the peers with high attendance during a given period.</li>\n<li>Up to 12 Lords Spiritual should be retained in a reformed House of Lords.</li></ul>\n<p>Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Reform_Bill_2012" title="House of Lords Reform Bill 2012">House of Lords Reform Bill 2012</a> on 27 June 2012<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> which built on proposals published on 17 May 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-House_of_Lords_reform_proposals_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-House_of_Lords_reform_proposals-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, this Bill was abandoned<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> by the Government on 6 August 2012 following opposition from within the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lords_Reform_Act_2014">House of Lords Reform Act 2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: House of Lords Reform Act 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A private members bill to introduce some reforms was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Dan_Byles" title="Dan Byles">Dan Byles</a> in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Reform_Act_2014" title="House of Lords Reform Act 2014">House of Lords Reform Act 2014</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-services.parliament.uk_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-services.parliament.uk-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Under the new law:\n</p>\n<ul><li>All peers can retire or resign from the chamber (prior to this only hereditary peers could disclaim their peerages).</li>\n<li>Peers can be disqualified for non-attendance.</li>\n<li>Peers can be removed for receiving prison sentences of a year or more.<sup id="cite_ref-services.parliament.uk_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-services.parliament.uk-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span id="House_of_Lords_.28Expulsion_and_Suspension.29_Act_2015"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lords_(Expulsion_and_Suspension)_Act_2015">House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_(Expulsion_and_Suspension)_Act_2015" title="House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015">House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015</a> authorised the House to expel or suspend members.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Lords_Spiritual_.28Women.29_Act_2015"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lords_Spiritual_(Women)_Act_2015">Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual_(Women)_Act_2015" title="Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015">Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_House_of_Lords" title="Women in the House of Lords">Women in the House of Lords</a></div>\n<p>This act makes provision to preferentially admit bishops of the Church of England who are women to the Lords Spiritual in the 10 years following its commencement.\n</p><p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Treweek" title="Rachel Treweek">Rachel Treweek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Gloucester" title="Bishop of Gloucester">Bishop of Gloucester</a>, became the first woman to sit as a <a href="/wiki/Lord_Spiritual" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Spiritual">Lord Spiritual</a> in the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overcrowding">Overcrowding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Overcrowding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The size of the House of Lords has varied greatly throughout its history. From about 220 members in the early 1700s,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> it increased to a record size of 1,330 in October 1999, before Lords reform reduced it to 669 by March 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2011, a cross-party group of former leading politicians, including many senior members of the House of Lords, called on the Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> to stop creating new peers. He had created 117 new peers since becoming prime minister in May 2010, a faster rate of elevation than any PM in British history. The expansion occurred while his government had tried (in vain) to reduce the size of the House of Commons by 50 members, from 650 to 600.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2014, despite there being a seating capacity of only around 230<sup id="cite_ref-BBCspace_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCspace-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> to 400<sup id="cite_ref-guard_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guard-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> on the benches in the Lords chamber, the House had 774 active members (plus 54 who were not entitled to attend or vote, having been suspended or granted leave of absence). This made the House of Lords the largest parliamentary chamber in any democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-guard_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guard-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In August 2014, former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Commons_(United_Kingdom)" title="Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)">Speaker of the House of Commons</a> <a href="/wiki/Betty_Boothroyd" title="Betty Boothroyd">Betty Boothroyd</a> requested that \xe2\x80\x9colder peers should retire gracefully\xe2\x80\x9d to ease the overcrowding in the House of Lords. She also criticised successive prime ministers for filling the second chamber with \xe2\x80\x9clobby fodder\xe2\x80\x9d in an attempt to help their policies become law. She made her remarks days before a new batch of peers were due to be created.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2015, following the creation of a further 45 peers in the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Honours_Lists#Dissolution_Honours" title="Crown Honours Lists">Dissolution Honours</a>, the total number of eligible members of the Lords increased to 826. In a report entitled <i>Does size matter?</i> the BBC said: "Increasingly, yes. Critics argue the House of Lords is the second largest legislature after the Chinese National People\'s Congress and dwarfs Upper Houses in other bi-cameral democracies such as the United States (100 senators), France (348 senators), Australia (76 senators) and India (250 members). The Lords is also larger than the Supreme People\'s Assembly of North Korea (687 members). [\xe2\x80\xa6 ] Peers grumble that there is not enough room to accommodate all of their colleagues in the Chamber, where there are only about 400 seats, and say they are constantly jostling for space \xe2\x80\x93 particularly during high-profile sittings", but added, "On the other hand, defenders of the Lords say that it does a vital job scrutinising legislation, a lot of which has come its way from the Commons in recent years".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_28_August_2015_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_28_August_2015-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_the_Government">Relationship with the Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Relationship with the Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The House of Lords does not control the term of the Prime Minister or of the Government.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Only the Lower House may force the Prime Minister to resign or call elections by <a href="/wiki/Motions_of_no_confidence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Motions of no confidence in the United Kingdom">passing a motion of no-confidence</a> or by <a href="/wiki/Loss_of_supply" title="Loss of supply">withdrawing supply</a>. Thus, the House of Lords\' oversight of the government is limited.\n</p><p>Most Cabinet ministers are from the House of Commons rather than the House of Lords. In particular, all Prime Ministers since 1902 have been members of the Lower House.<sup id="cite_ref-wasson_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wasson-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>, who became Prime Minister in 1963 whilst still an Earl, disclaimed his peerage and was elected to the Commons soon after his term began.) In recent history, it has been very rare for major cabinet positions (except Lord Chancellor and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Leader of the House of Lords">Leader of the House of Lords</a>) to have been filled by peers.\n</p><p>Exceptions include <a href="/wiki/Peter_Carington,_6th_Baron_Carrington" title="Peter Carington, 6th Baron Carrington">Lord Carrington</a>, who was the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> between 1979 and 1982, <a href="/wiki/David_Young,_Baron_Young_of_Graffham" title="David Young, Baron Young of Graffham">Lord Young of Graffham</a> (<a href="/wiki/Minister_without_Portfolio" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister without Portfolio">Minister without Portfolio</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Employment" title="Secretary of State for Employment">Secretary of State for Employment</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Trade_and_Industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Trade and Industry">Secretary of State for Trade and Industry</a> from 1984 to 1989), <a href="/wiki/Valerie_Amos,_Baroness_Amos" title="Valerie Amos, Baroness Amos">Baroness Amos</a>, who served as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_International_Development" title="Secretary of State for International Development">Secretary of State for International Development</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Mandelson" title="Peter Mandelson">Lord Mandelson</a>, who served as <a href="/wiki/First_Secretary_of_State" title="First Secretary of State">First Secretary of State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Business,_Innovation_and_Skills" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Business, Innovation and Skills">Secretary of State for Business, Innovation and Skills</a> and <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a>. <a href="/wiki/George_Robertson,_Baron_Robertson_of_Port_Ellen" title="George Robertson, Baron Robertson of Port Ellen">Lord Robertson of Port Ellen</a> was briefly a peer whilst serving as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Secretary of State for Defence</a> before resigning to take up the post of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_General_of_NATO" title="Secretary General of NATO">Secretary General of NATO</a>. From 1999 to 2010 the <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Attorney General for England and Wales">Attorney General for England and Wales</a> was a Member of the House of Lords; the most recent was <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Scotland" title="Patricia Scotland">Baroness Scotland of Asthal</a>.\n</p><p>The House of Lords remains a source for junior ministers and members of government. Like the House of Commons, the Lords also has a <a href="/wiki/Captain_of_the_Honourable_Corps_of_Gentlemen-at-Arms" title="Captain of the Honourable Corps of Gentlemen-at-Arms">Government Chief Whip</a> as well as several Junior Whips. Where a government department is not represented by a minister in the Lords or one is not available, government whips will act as spokesmen for them.<sup id="cite_ref-companion_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companion-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_functions">Legislative functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Legislative functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament_(UK)" title="Act of Parliament (UK)">Act of Parliament (UK)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Westminster,_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/300px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" width="300" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/450px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/600px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="1261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Westminster,_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The House of Lords meets in a chamber in the Palace of Westminster.</div></div></div>\n<p>Legislation, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Money_bills" class="mw-redirect" title="Money bills">money bills</a>, may be introduced in either House.\n</p><p>The House of Lords debates legislation, and has power to amend or reject bills. However, the power of the Lords to reject a bill passed by the House of Commons is severely restricted by the Parliament Acts. Under those Acts, certain types of bills may be presented for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> without the consent of the House of Lords (i.e. the Commons can override the Lords\' veto). The House of Lords cannot delay a money bill (a bill that, in the view of the Speaker of the House of Commons, solely concerns national taxation or public funds) for more than one month.\n</p><p>Other public bills cannot be delayed by the House of Lords for more than two parliamentary sessions, or one calendar year. These provisions, however, only apply to public bills that originate in the House of Commons, and cannot have the effect of extending a parliamentary term beyond five years. A further restriction is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional convention</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Convention" title="Salisbury Convention">Salisbury Convention</a>, which means that the House of Lords does not oppose legislation promised in the Government\'s election manifesto.\n</p><p>By a custom that prevailed even before the Parliament Acts, the House of Lords is further restrained insofar as financial bills are concerned. The House of Lords may neither originate a bill concerning taxation or Supply (supply of treasury or exchequer funds), nor amend a bill so as to insert a taxation or Supply-related provision. (The House of Commons, however, often waives its privileges and allows the Upper House to make amendments with financial implications.) Moreover, the Upper House may not amend any Supply Bill. The House of Lords formerly maintained the absolute power to reject a bill relating to revenue or Supply, but this power was curtailed by the Parliament Acts, as aforementioned.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_judicial_role">Former judicial role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Former judicial role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judicial_functions_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Judicial functions of the House of Lords">Judicial functions of the House of Lords</a></div>\n<p>Historically, the House of Lords held several judicial functions. Most notably, until 2009 the House of Lords served as the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_last_resort" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of last resort">court of last resort</a> for most instances of UK law. Since 1 October 2009 this role is now held by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Supreme Court of the United Kingdom">Supreme Court of the United Kingdom</a>.\n</p><p>The Lords\' judicial functions originated from the ancient role of the <a href="/wiki/Curia_Regis" class="mw-redirect" title="Curia Regis">Curia Regis</a> as a body that addressed the petitions of the King\'s subjects. The functions were exercised not by the whole House, but by a committee of "Law Lords". The bulk of the House\'s judicial business was conducted by the twelve <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Appeal_in_Ordinary" title="Lords of Appeal in Ordinary">Lords of Appeal in Ordinary</a>, who were specifically appointed for this purpose under the <a href="/wiki/Appellate_Jurisdiction_Act_1876" title="Appellate Jurisdiction Act 1876">Appellate Jurisdiction Act 1876</a>.\n</p><p>The judicial functions could also be exercised by Lords of Appeal (other members of the House who happened to have held high judicial office). No Lord of Appeal in Ordinary or Lord of Appeal could sit judicially beyond the age of seventy-five. The judicial business of the Lords was supervised by the Senior Lord of Appeal in Ordinary and their deputy, the Second Senior Lord of Appeal in Ordinary.\n</p><p>The jurisdiction of the House of Lords extended, in civil and in criminal cases, to appeals from the courts of England and Wales, and of Northern Ireland. From Scotland, appeals were possible only in civil cases; Scotland\'s <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justiciary" title="High Court of Justiciary">High Court of Justiciary</a> is the highest court in criminal matters. The House of Lords was not the United Kingdom\'s only court of last resort; in some cases, the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_Committee_of_the_Privy_Council" title="Judicial Committee of the Privy Council">Judicial Committee of the Privy Council</a> performs such a function. The jurisdiction of the Privy Council in the United Kingdom, however, is relatively restricted; it encompasses appeals from <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical">ecclesiastical</a> courts, disputes under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_Disqualification_Act_1975" title="House of Commons Disqualification Act 1975">House of Commons Disqualification Act 1975</a>, and a few other minor matters. Issues related to <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> were transferred from the Privy Council to the Supreme Court in 2009.\n</p><p>The twelve Law Lords did not all hear every case; rather, after World War II cases were heard by panels known as Appellate Committees, each of which normally consisted of five members (selected by the Senior Lord). An Appellate Committee hearing an important case could consist of more than five members. Though Appellate Committees met in separate committee rooms, judgement was given in the Lords Chamber itself. No further appeal lay from the House of Lords, although the House of Lords could refer a "preliminary question" to the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> in cases involving an element of <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a>, and a case could be brought at the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> if the House of Lords did not provide a satisfactory remedy in cases where the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> was relevant.\n</p><p>A distinct judicial function\xe2\x80\x94one in which the whole House used to participate\xe2\x80\x94is that of trying <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachments</a>. Impeachments were brought by the House of Commons, and tried in the House of Lords; a conviction required only a majority of the Lords voting. Impeachments, however, are to all intents and purposes obsolete; the last impeachment was that of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dundas,_1st_Viscount_Melville" title="Henry Dundas, 1st Viscount Melville">Henry Dundas, 1st Viscount Melville</a>, in 1806.\n</p><p>Similarly, the House of Lords was once the court that tried peers charged with high <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a> or <a href="/wiki/Felony" title="Felony">felony</a>. The House would be presided over not by the Lord Chancellor, but by the <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Steward" title="Lord High Steward">Lord High Steward</a>, an official especially appointed for the occasion of the trial. If Parliament was not in session, then peers could be tried in a separate court, known as the Lord High Steward\'s Court. Only peers, their wives, and their widows (unless remarried) were entitled to <a href="/wiki/List_of_trials_of_peers_in_the_House_of_Lords" title="List of trials of peers in the House of Lords">such trials</a>; the Lords Spiritual were tried in ecclesiastical courts. In 1948, the right of peers to be tried in such special courts was abolished; now, they are tried in the regular courts.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The last such trial in the House was of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Russell,_26th_Baron_de_Clifford" title="Edward Russell, 26th Baron de Clifford">Edward Russell, 26th Baron de Clifford</a>, in 1935. An illustrative dramatisation circa 1928 of a trial of a peer (the fictional Duke of Denver) on a charge of murder (a felony) is portrayed in the 1972 BBC Television adaption of <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_L._Sayers" title="Dorothy L. Sayers">Dorothy L. Sayers</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Lord_Peter_Wimsey" title="Lord Peter Wimsey">Lord Peter Wimsey</a> mystery <i><a href="/wiki/Clouds_of_Witness" title="Clouds of Witness">Clouds of Witness</a></i>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Reform_Act_2005" title="Constitutional Reform Act 2005">Constitutional Reform Act 2005</a> resulted in the creation of a separate Supreme Court of the United Kingdom, to which the judicial function of the House of Lords, and some of the judicial functions of the Judicial Committee of the Privy Council, were transferred. In addition, the office of Lord Chancellor was reformed by the act, removing his ability to act as both a government minister and a judge. This was motivated in part by concerns about the historical admixture of legislative, judicial, and executive power. The new Supreme Court is located at <a href="/wiki/Middlesex_Guildhall" title="Middlesex Guildhall">Middlesex Guildhall</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em Kingdom</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-bottom:0;border-bottom:1px solid #B31B1B;text-align:center;">History</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-top:0;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_peerage" title="History of the British peerage">Overview</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Privilege_of_peerage" title="Privilege of peerage">Privileges</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robes_of_the_British_peerage" title="Robes of the British peerage">Robes</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#B31B1B;padding-top:0.15em;">\n<a class="mw-selflink selflink"><span style="color:white">House of Lords</span></a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:none;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Royal Coat of Arms of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="15" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/24px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:British_politics" title="Portal:British politics">British politics&#32;portal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/24px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x,
