href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Peerage" title="Template:Peerage"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Peerage" title="Template talk:Peerage"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Peerage&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lords_Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Lords Spiritual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</a></div>\n<p>Members of the House of Lords who sit by virtue of their ecclesiastical offices are known as Lords Spiritual.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Formerly, the Lords Spiritual were the majority in the English House of Lords,<sup id="cite_ref-shell1_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shell1-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> comprising the church\'s <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishops</a>, (diocesan) bishops, <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbots</a>, and those <a href="/wiki/Prior" title="Prior">priors</a> who were entitled to wear a <a href="/wiki/Mitre" title="Mitre">mitre</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>\'s highpoint in 1539, only the archbishops and bishops continued to attend, as the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> had just disproved of<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and suppressed the positions of abbot and prior. In 1642, during the few Lords\' gatherings convened during <a href="/wiki/English_Interregnum" class="mw-redirect" title="English Interregnum">English Interregnum</a> which saw periodic war, the Lords Spiritual were excluded altogether, but they returned under the <a href="/wiki/Clergy_Act_1661" title="Clergy Act 1661">Clergy Act 1661</a>.\n</p><p>The number of Lords Spiritual was further restricted by the <a href="/wiki/Bishopric_of_Manchester_Act_1847" title="Bishopric of Manchester Act 1847">Bishopric of Manchester Act 1847</a>, and by later Acts. The Lords Spiritual can now number no more than 26; these are the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">Bishop of London</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Durham" title="Bishop of Durham">Bishop of Durham</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Winchester" title="Bishop of Winchester">Bishop of Winchester</a> (who sit by right regardless of seniority) and the 21 longest-serving bishops from other <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">dioceses</a> in the Church of England<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> (excluding the dioceses of <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Sodor_and_Man" title="Diocese of Sodor and Man">Sodor and Man</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe">Gibraltar in Europe</a>, as these lie entirely outside the United Kingdom).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Following a change to the law in 2014 to allow women to be ordained bishops, the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual_(Women)_Act_2015" title="Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015">Lords Spiritual (Women) Act 2015</a> was passed, which provides that whenever a vacancy arises among the Lords Spiritual during the ten years following the Act coming into force, the vacancy has to be filled by a woman, if one is eligible. This does not apply to the five bishops who sit by right.\n</p><p>The current Lords Spiritual represent only the Church of England. Bishops of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> historically sat in the Parliament of Scotland but were finally excluded in 1689 (after a number of previous exclusions) when the Church of Scotland became permanently Presbyterian. There are no longer <a href="/wiki/Bishops_in_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="Bishops in the Church of Scotland">bishops in the Church of Scotland</a> in the traditional sense of the word, and that Church has never sent members to sit in the Westminster House of Lords. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> did obtain representation in the House of Lords after the union of Ireland and Great Britain in 1801.\n</p><p>Of the Church of Ireland\'s ecclesiastics, four (one archbishop and three bishops) were to sit at any one time, with the members rotating at the end of every parliamentary session (which normally lasted about one year). The Church of Ireland, however, was <a href="/wiki/Disestablishment_of_the_Church_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Disestablishment of the Church of Ireland">disestablished</a> in 1871, and thereafter ceased to be represented by Lords Spiritual. Bishops of Welsh sees in the Church of England originally sat in the House of Lords (after 1847, only if their seniority within the Church entitled them to), but the <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> ceased to be a part of the Church of England in 1920 and was simultaneously <a href="/wiki/Disestablishment_of_the_Church_in_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Disestablishment of the Church in Wales">disestablished</a> in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Accordingly, bishops of the Church in Wales were no longer eligible to be appointed to the House as bishops of the Church of England, but those already appointed remained.\n</p><p>Other ecclesiastics have sat in the House of Lords as Lords Temporal in recent times: <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbi" title="Chief Rabbi">Chief Rabbi</a> <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Jakobovits" class="mw-redirect" title="Immanuel Jakobovits">Immanuel Jakobovits</a> was appointed to the House of Lords (with the consent of the Queen, who acted on the advice of Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher), as was his successor Chief Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Sacks" title="Jonathan Sacks">Jonathan Sacks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julia_Neuberger" title="Julia Neuberger">Julia Neuberger</a> is the Senior Rabbi to the West London Synagogue. In recognition of his work at reconciliation and in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">peace process</a> in Northern Ireland, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Armagh_(Church_of_Ireland)" title="Archbishop of Armagh (Church of Ireland)">Archbishop of Armagh</a> (the senior Anglican bishop in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>), <a href="/wiki/Robin_Eames" title="Robin Eames">Robin Eames</a>, was appointed to the Lords by <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>. Other clergymen appointed include <a href="/wiki/Donald_Soper" class="mw-redirect" title="Donald Soper">Donald Soper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Beaumont" class="mw-redirect" title="Timothy Beaumont">Timothy Beaumont</a>, and some Scottish clerics.\n</p><p>There have been no Roman Catholic clergymen appointed, though it was rumoured that <a href="/wiki/Basil_Hume" title="Basil Hume">Cardinal Basil Hume</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Cormac_Murphy_O%27Connor" class="mw-redirect" title="Cormac Murphy O&#39;Connor">Cormac Murphy O\'Connor</a> were offered peerages, by <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, Margaret Thatcher and Tony Blair respectively, but declined. Hume later accepted the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit_(Commonwealth)" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Merit (Commonwealth)">Order of Merit</a>, a personal appointment of the Queen, shortly before his death. O\'Connor said he had his maiden speech ready, but Roman Catholics who have received Holy Orders are prohibited by <a href="/wiki/Canon_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon Law">Canon Law</a> from holding major offices connected with any government other than the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>.\n</p><p>Former Archbishops of Canterbury, having reverted to the status of bishop but who are no longer diocesans, are invariably given life peerages and sit as Lords Temporal.\n</p><p>By custom at least one of the Bishops reads prayers in each legislative day (a role taken by the chaplain in the Commons).<sup id="cite_ref-shell1_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shell1-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> They often speak in debates; in 2004 <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Williams" title="Rowan Williams">Rowan Williams</a>, the Archbishop of Canterbury, opened a debate into sentencing legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-shell1_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shell1-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Church_of_England_Measures" title="List of Church of England Measures">Measures</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bill (proposed law)">proposed laws</a> of the Church of England) must be put before the Lords, and the Lords Spiritual have a role in ensuring that this takes place.<sup id="cite_ref-shell1_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shell1-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lords_Temporal">Lords Temporal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Lords Temporal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since the Dissolution of the Monasteries, the Lords Temporal have been the most numerous group in the House of Lords. Unlike the Lords Spiritual, they may be publicly partisan, aligning themselves with one or another of the political parties that dominate the House of Commons. Publicly non-partisan Lords are called <a href="/wiki/Crossbencher" title="Crossbencher">crossbenchers</a>. Originally, the Lords Temporal included several hundred hereditary peers (that is, those whose peerages may be inherited), who ranked variously as <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">dukes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marquess" title="Marquess">marquesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viscount" title="Viscount">viscounts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">barons</a> (as well as Scottish <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Lords of Parliament">Lords of Parliament</a>). Such hereditary dignities can be created by the Crown; in modern times this is done on the advice of the Prime Minister of the day (except in the case of members of the Royal Family).\n</p><p>Holders of <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_Scotland" title="Peerage of Scotland">Scottish</a> and Irish peerages were not always permitted to sit in the Lords. When Scotland united with England to form <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> in 1707, it was provided that the Scottish hereditary peers would only be able to elect 16 <a href="/wiki/Representative_peer" title="Representative peer">representative peers</a> to sit in the House of Lords; the term of a representative was to extend until the next general election. A similar provision was enacted when Ireland merged with Great Britain in 1801 to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>; the Irish peers were allowed to elect 28 representatives, who were to retain office for life. Elections for Irish representatives ended in 1922, when most of Ireland became an independent state; elections for Scottish representatives ended with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Peerage_Act_1963" title="Peerage Act 1963">Peerage Act 1963</a>, under which all Scottish peers obtained seats in the Upper House.\n</p><p>In 1999, the Labour government brought forward the House of Lords Act removing the right of several hundred hereditary peers to sit in the House. The Act provided, as a measure intended to be temporary, that 92 people would continue to sit in the Lords by virtue of hereditary peerages, and this is still in effect.\n</p><p>Of the 92, two remain in the House of Lords because they hold royal offices connected with Parliament: the <a href="/wiki/Earl_Marshal#England" title="Earl Marshal">Earl Marshal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Great_Chamberlain" title="Lord Great Chamberlain">Lord Great Chamberlain</a>. Of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_elected_hereditary_peers_under_the_House_of_Lords_Act_1999" title="List of elected hereditary peers under the House of Lords Act 1999">remaining ninety peers sitting in the Lords by virtue of a hereditary peerage</a>, 15 are elected by the whole House and 75 are chosen by fellow hereditary peers in the House of Lords, grouped by party. (If a hereditary peerage holder is given a life peerage, he or she becomes a member of the House of Lords without a need for a by-election.) The exclusion of other hereditary peers removed <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Charles, Prince of Wales</a> (who is also <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Chester" title="Earl of Chester">Earl of Chester</a>) and all other Royal Peers, including <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh</a>; <a href="/wiki/Prince_Andrew,_Duke_of_York" title="Prince Andrew, Duke of York">Prince Andrew, Duke of York</a>; <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Earl_of_Wessex" title="Prince Edward, Earl of Wessex">Prince Edward, Earl of Wessex</a>; <a href="/wiki/Prince_Richard,_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Prince Richard, Duke of Gloucester">Prince Richard, Duke of Gloucester</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Duke_of_Kent" title="Prince Edward, Duke of Kent">Prince Edward, Duke of Kent</a>.\n</p><p>The number of peers to be chosen by a political group reflects the proportion of hereditary peers that belonged to that group (see <a href="#Current_composition">current composition</a> below) in 1999. When an elected hereditary peer dies, a <a href="/wiki/By-elections_to_the_House_of_Lords" title="By-elections to the House of Lords">by-election</a> is held, with a variant of the <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">Alternative Vote</a> system being used. If the recently deceased hereditary peer had been elected by the whole House, then so is his or her replacement; a hereditary peer elected by a specific political group (including the non-aligned crossbenchers) is replaced by a vote of the hereditary peers already elected to the Lords belonging to that political group (whether elected by that group or by the whole house).\n</p><p>Until 2009, the Lords Temporal also included the Lords of Appeal in Ordinary, a group of individuals appointed to the House of Lords so that they could exercise its judicial functions. Lords of Appeal in Ordinary, more commonly known as Law Lords, were first appointed under the Appellate Jurisdiction Act 1876. They were selected by the Prime Minister of the day, but were formally appointed by the Sovereign. A Lord of Appeal in Ordinary had to retire at the age of 70, or, if his or her term was extended by the government, at the age of 75; after reaching such an age, the Law Lord could not hear any further cases in the House of Lords.\n</p><p>The number of Lords of Appeal in Ordinary (excluding those who were no longer able to hear cases because of age restrictions) was limited to twelve, but could be changed by <a href="/wiki/Statutory_instrument" title="Statutory instrument">statutory instrument</a>. By a convention of the House, Lords of Appeal in Ordinary did not take part in debates on new legislation, so as to maintain judicial independence. Lords of Appeal in Ordinary held their seats in the House of Lords for life, remaining as members even after reaching the judicial retirement age of 70 or 75. Former Lord Chancellors and holders of other high judicial office could also sit as Law Lords under the Appellate Jurisdiction Act, although in practice this right was only rarely exercised.\n</p><p>Under the Constitutional Reform Act 2005, the Lords of Appeal in Ordinary when the Act came into effect in 2009 became judges of the new Supreme Court of the United Kingdom and were then barred from sitting or voting in the House of Lords until they had retired as judges. One of the main justifications for the new Supreme Court was to establish a separation of powers between the judiciary and the legislature. It is therefore unlikely that future appointees to the Supreme Court of the United Kingdom will be made Lords of Appeal in Ordinary.\n</p><p>The largest group of Lords Temporal, and indeed of the whole House, are life peers. Life peerages rank only as barons or baronesses, and are created under the Life Peerages Act 1958. Like all other peers, life peers are created by the Sovereign, who acts on the advice of the Prime Minister or the House of Lords Appointments Commission. By convention, however, the Prime Minister allows leaders of other parties to nominate some life peers, so as to maintain a political balance in the House of Lords. Moreover, some non-party life peers (the number being determined by the Prime Minister) are nominated by the independent House of Lords Appointments Commission.\n</p><p>In 2000, the government announced it would set up an Independent Appointments Commission, under <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Stevenson,_Baron_Stevenson_of_Coddenham" title="Dennis Stevenson, Baron Stevenson of Coddenham">Dennis, Lord Stevenson of Coddenham</a>, to select fifteen so-called "<a href="/wiki/People%27s_peer" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s peer">people\'s peers</a>" for life peerages. However, when the choices were announced in April 2001, from a list of 3,000 applicants, the choices were treated with criticism in the media, as all were distinguished in their field, and none were "ordinary people" as some had originally hoped.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualifications">Qualifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Qualifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_House_of_Lords" title="Women in the House of Lords">Women in the House of Lords</a></div>\n<p>Several different qualifications apply for membership of the House of Lords. No person may sit in the House of Lords if under the age of 21.<sup id="cite_ref-standing_orders1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-standing_orders1-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, only United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationality_law" title="Irish nationality law">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_citizen" title="Commonwealth citizen">Commonwealth</a> citizens may sit in the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The nationality restrictions were previously more stringent: under the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a>, and prior to the <a href="/wiki/British_Nationality_Act_1948" title="British Nationality Act 1948">British Nationality Act 1948</a>, only natural-born subjects qualified.\n</p><p>Additionally, some bankruptcy-related restrictions apply to members of the Upper House. A person may not sit in the House of Lords if he or she is the subject of a Bankruptcy Restrictions Order (applicable in England and Wales only), or if he or she is adjudged bankrupt (in Northern Ireland), or if his or her estate is sequestered (in Scotland). A final restriction bars an individual convicted of <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a> from sitting in the House of Lords until completing his or her full term of imprisonment. An exception applies, however, if the individual convicted of high treason receives a full pardon. Note that an individual serving a prison sentence for an offence other than high treason is <i>not</i> automatically disqualified.\n</p><p>Women were excluded from the House of Lords until the Life Peerages Act 1958,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> passed to address the declining number of active members, made possible the creation of peerages for life. Women were immediately eligible and four were among the first life peers appointed. However, hereditary peeresses continued to be excluded until the passage of the Peerage Act 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Since the passage of the House of Lords Act 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> hereditary peeresses remain eligible for election to the Upper House; there is one (<a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Mar,_31st_Countess_of_Mar" title="Margaret of Mar, 31st Countess of Mar">Margaret of Mar, 31st Countess of Mar</a>) among the 90 hereditary peers who continue to sit.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cash_for_Peerages">Cash for Peerages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cash for Peerages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Honours_(Prevention_of_Abuses)_Act_1925" title="Honours (Prevention of Abuses) Act 1925">Honours (Prevention of Abuses) Act 1925</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> made it illegal for a peerage, or other honour, to be bought or sold. Nonetheless, there have been repeated allegations that life peerages (and thus membership of the House of Lords) have been made available to major political donors in exchange for donations. The most prominent case, the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Cash_for_Honours" title="Cash for Honours">Cash for Honours</a> scandal, saw a police investigation, with no charges being brought. A 2015 study found that of 303 people nominated for peerages in the period 2005\xe2\x80\x9314, a total of 211 were former senior figures within politics (including former MPs), or were non-political appointments. Of the remaining 92 political appointments from outside public life, 27 had made significant donations to political parties. The authors concluded firstly that nominees from outside public life were much more likely to have made large gifts than peers nominated after prior political or public service. They also found that significant donors to parties were far more likely to be nominated for peerages than other party members.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_from_House_membership">Removal from House membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Removal from House membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Traditionally there was no mechanism by which members could resign or be removed from the House of Lords (compare the situation as regards <a href="/wiki/Resignation_from_the_British_House_of_Commons" title="Resignation from the British House of Commons">resignation from the House of Commons</a>). The Peerage Act 1963 permitted a person to disclaim their newly inherited peerage (within certain time limits); this meant that such a person could effectively renounce their membership of the Lords. This might be done in order to remain or become qualified to sit in the House of Commons, as in the case of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a> (formerly the second <a href="/wiki/Viscount_Stansgate" title="Viscount Stansgate">Viscount Stansgate</a>), who had campaigned for such a change.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Reform_Act_2014" title="House of Lords Reform Act 2014">House of Lords Reform Act 2014</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> made provision for members\' resignation from the House, removal for non-attendance, and automatic expulsion upon conviction for a serious criminal offence (if resulting in a jail sentence of at least one year). In June 2015, under the House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> the House\'s Standing Orders may provide for the expulsion or suspension of a member upon a resolution of the House.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Officers">Officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Traditionally the House of Lords did not elect its own speaker, unlike the House of Commons; rather, the <i>ex officio</i> presiding officer was the Lord Chancellor. With the passage of the Constitutional Reform Act 2005, the post of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Speaker" title="Lord Speaker">Lord Speaker</a> was created, a position to which a peer is elected by the House and subsequently appointed by the Crown. The first Lord Speaker, elected on 4 May 2006, was <a href="/wiki/Helene_Hayman" class="mw-redirect" title="Helene Hayman">Helene Hayman</a>, a former Labour peer. As the Speaker is expected to be an impartial presiding officer, Hayman resigned from the Labour Party.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Frances_D%27Souza" class="mw-redirect" title="Frances D&#39;Souza">Frances D\'Souza</a> was elected as the second Lord Speaker, replacing Hayman in September 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> D\'Souza was in turn succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Fowler" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Fowler">Norman Fowler</a> in September 2016, the incumbent Lord Speaker.\n</p><p>This reform of the post of Lord Chancellor was made due to the perceived constitutional anomalies inherent in the role. The Lord Chancellor was not only the Speaker of the House of Lords, but also a member of the Cabinet; his or her department, formerly the Lord Chancellor\'s Department, is now called the Ministry of Justice. The Lord Chancellor is no longer the head of the judiciary of England and Wales. Hitherto, the Lord Chancellor was part of all three branches of government: the legislative, the executive, and the judicial.\n</p><p>The overlap of the legislative and executive roles is a characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, as the entire cabinet consists of members of the House of Commons or the House of Lords; however, in June 2003, the Blair Government announced its intention to abolish the post of Lord Chancellor because of the office\'s mixed executive and judicial responsibilities. The abolition of the office was rejected by the House of Lords, and the Constitutional Reform Act 2005 was thus amended to preserve the office of Lord Chancellor. The Act no longer guarantees that the office holder of Lord Chancellor is the presiding officer of the House of Lords, and therefore allows the House of Lords to elect a speaker of their own.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Pepys,_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg/220px-Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg/330px-Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg/440px-Charles_Pepys%2C_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3187" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Pepys,_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham_by_Charles_Robert_Leslie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Pepys,_1st_Earl_of_Cottenham" title="Charles Pepys, 1st Earl of Cottenham">Charles Pepys</a> as Lord Chancellor. The Lord Chancellor wore black and gold robes whilst presiding over the House of Lords.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Lord Speaker may be replaced as presiding officer by one of his or her deputies. The <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_Committees_(House_of_Lords)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman of Committees (House of Lords)">Chairman of Committees</a>, the Principal Deputy Chairman of Committees, and several Chairmen are all deputies to the Lord Speaker, and are all appointed by the House of Lords itself at the beginning of each session. By custom, the Crown appoints each Chairman, Principal Deputy Chairman and Deputy Chairman to the additional office of Deputy Speaker of the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> There was previously no legal requirement that the Lord Chancellor or a Deputy Speaker be a member of the House of Lords (though the same has long been customary).\n</p><p>Whilst presiding over the House of Lords, the Lord Chancellor traditionally wore ceremonial black and gold robes. Robes of black and gold are now worn by the Lord Chancellor and Secretary of State for Justice in the House of Commons, on ceremonial occasions. This is no longer a requirement for the Lord Speaker except for State occasions outside of the chamber. The Speaker or Deputy Speaker sits on the <a href="/wiki/Woolsack" title="Woolsack">Woolsack</a>, a large red seat stuffed with wool, at the front of the Lords Chamber.\n</p><p>When the House of Lords resolves itself into committee (see below), the Chairman of Committees or a Deputy Chairman of Committees presides, not from the Woolsack, but from a chair at the Table of the House. The presiding officer has little power compared to the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_Kingdom_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Speaker of the United Kingdom House of Commons">Speaker of the House of Commons</a>. He or she only acts as the mouthpiece of the House, performing duties such as announcing the results of votes. This is because, unlike in the House of Commons where all statements are directed to "Mr/Madam Speaker", in the House of Lords they are directed to "My Lords"; i.e., the entire body of the House.\n</p><p>The Lord Speaker or Deputy Speaker cannot determine which members may speak, or discipline members for violating the rules of the House; these measures may be taken only by the House itself. Unlike the politically neutral Speaker of the House of Commons, the Lord Chancellor and Deputy Speakers originally remained members of their respective parties, and were permitted to participate in debate; however, this is no longer true of the new role of Lord Speaker.\n</p><p>Another officer of the body is the Leader of the House of Lords, a peer selected by the Prime Minister. The Leader of the House is responsible for steering Government bills through the House of Lords, and is a member of the Cabinet. The Leader also advises the House on proper procedure when necessary, but such advice is merely informal, rather than official and binding. A Deputy Leader is also appointed by the Prime Minister, and takes the place of an absent or unavailable leader.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Clerk_of_the_Parliaments" title="Clerk of the Parliaments">Clerk of the Parliaments</a> is the chief clerk and officer of the House of Lords (but is not a member of the House itself). The Clerk, who is appointed by the Crown, advises the presiding officer on the rules of the House, signs orders and official communications, endorses bills, and is the keeper of the official records of both Houses of Parliament. Moreover, the Clerk of the Parliaments is responsible for arranging by-elections of hereditary peers when necessary. The deputies of the Clerk of the Parliaments (the Clerk Assistant and the Reading Clerk) are appointed by the Lord Speaker, subject to the House\'s approval.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Rod" title="Black Rod">Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod</a> is also an officer of the House; he takes his title from the symbol of his office, a black rod. Black Rod (as the Gentleman Usher is normally known) is responsible for ceremonial arrangements, is in charge of the House\'s doorkeepers, and may (upon the order of the House) take action to end disorder or disturbance in the Chamber. Black Rod also holds the office of <a href="/wiki/Serjeant-at-Arms" class="mw-redirect" title="Serjeant-at-Arms">Serjeant-at-Arms</a> of the House of Lords, and in this capacity attends upon the Lord Speaker. The Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod\'s duties may be delegated to the Yeoman Usher of the Black Rod or to the Assistant Serjeant-at-Arms.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Procedure">Procedure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Procedure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg/220px-Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg/330px-Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg/440px-Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chamber_of_the_House_of_Lords_benches.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Benches in the chamber are coloured red. In contrast, the House of Commons is decorated in green.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg/220px-Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg/330px-Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg/440px-Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg 2x" data-file-width="781" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thrones_in_the_House_of_Lords_c1902.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The royal thrones, c.&#160;1902. Note that the Sovereign\'s throne (on left) is raised slightly higher than the consort\'s.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Parliament_in_the_United_Kingdom#Stages_of_a_bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts of Parliament in the United Kingdom">Stages of a bill section in Acts of Parliament in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The House of Lords and the House of Commons assemble in the Palace of Westminster. The Lords Chamber is lavishly decorated, in contrast with the more modestly furnished Commons Chamber. Benches in the Lords Chamber are coloured red. The Woolsack is at the front of the Chamber; the Government sit on benches on the right of the Woolsack, while members of the Opposition sit on the left. Crossbenchers, sit on the benches immediately opposite the Woolsack.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Lords Chamber is the site of many formal ceremonies, the most famous of which is the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a>, held at the beginning of each new parliamentary session. During the State Opening, the <a href="/wiki/British_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchy">Sovereign</a>, seated on the Throne in the Lords Chamber and in the presence of both Houses of Parliament, delivers a speech outlining the Government\'s agenda for the upcoming parliamentary session.\n</p><p>In the House of Lords, members need not seek the recognition of the presiding officer before speaking, as is done in the House of Commons. If two or more Lords simultaneously rise to speak, the House decides which one is to be heard by acclamation, or, if necessary, by voting on a motion. Often, however, the Leader of the House will suggest an order, which is thereafter generally followed. Speeches in the House of Lords are addressed to the House as a whole ("My Lords") rather than to the presiding officer alone (as is the custom in the Lower House). Members may not refer to each other in the second person (as "you"), but rather use third person forms such as "the noble Duke", "the noble Earl", "the noble Lord", "my noble friend", "The most Reverend Primate", etc.\n</p><p>Each member may make no more than one speech on a motion, except that the mover of the motion may make one speech at the beginning of the debate and another at the end. Speeches are not subject to any time limits in the House; however, the House may put an end to a speech by approving a motion "that the noble Lord be no longer heard". It is also possible for the House to end the debate entirely, by approving a motion "that the Question be now put". This procedure is known as <a href="/wiki/Cloture" title="Cloture">Closure</a>, and is extremely rare.\n</p><p>Once all speeches on a motion have concluded, or Closure invoked, the motion may be put to a vote. The House first votes by <a href="/wiki/Voice_vote" title="Voice vote">voice vote</a>; the Lord Speaker or Deputy Speaker puts the question, and the Lords respond either "content" (in favour of the motion) or "not content" (against the motion). The presiding officer then announces the result of the voice vote, but if his assessment is challenged by any Lord, a recorded vote known as a <a href="/wiki/Division_(vote)" class="mw-redirect" title="Division (vote)">division</a> follows.\n</p><p>Members of the House enter one of two lobbies (the <i>content</i> lobby or the <i>not-content</i> lobby) on either side of the Chamber, where their names are recorded by clerks. At each lobby are two Tellers (themselves members of the House) who count the votes of the Lords. The Lord Speaker may not take part in the vote. Once the division concludes, the Tellers provide the results thereof to the presiding officer, who then announces them to the House.\n</p><p>If there is an equality of votes, the motion is decided according to the following principles: legislation may proceed in its present form, unless there is a majority in favour of amending or rejecting it; any other motions are rejected, unless there is a majority in favour of approving it. The quorum of the House of Lords is just three members for a general or procedural vote, and 30 members for a vote on legislation. If fewer than three or 30 members (as appropriate) are present, the division is invalid.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disciplinary_powers">Disciplinary powers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Disciplinary powers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By contrast with the House of Commons, the House of Lords has not until recently had an established procedure for putting sanctions on its members. When a <a href="/wiki/2009_cash_for_influence_scandal" title="2009 cash for influence scandal">cash for influence scandal</a> was referred to the Committee of Privileges in January 2009, the Leader of the House of Lords also asked the Privileges Committee to report on what sanctions the House had against its members.<sup id="cite_ref-Reference_to_committee_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reference_to_committee-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> After seeking advice from the Attorney General for England and Wales and the former Lord Chancellor <a href="/wiki/James_Mackay,_Baron_Mackay_of_Clashfern" title="James Mackay, Baron Mackay of Clashfern">James, Lord Mackay of Clashfern</a>, the committee decided that the House "possessed an inherent power" to suspend errant members, although not to withhold a <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_peer#Writs_of_summons" title="Hereditary peer">writ of summons</a> nor to expel a member permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-Powers_of_suspension_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powers_of_suspension-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> When the House subsequently suspended <a href="/wiki/Peter_Truscott,_Baron_Truscott" title="Peter Truscott, Baron Truscott">Peter, Lord Truscott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Taylor,_Baron_Taylor_of_Blackburn" title="Tom Taylor, Baron Taylor of Blackburn">Tom, Lord Taylor of Blackburn</a> for their role in the scandal, they were the first to meet this fate since 1642.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_and_Truscott_suspended_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_and_Truscott_suspended-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent changes have expanded the disciplinary powers of the House. Section 3 of the House of Lords Reform Act 2014 now provides that any member of the House of Lords convicted of a crime and sentenced to imprisonment for more than one year loses their seat. The House of Lords (Expulsion and Suspension) Act 2015 allows the House to set up procedures to suspend, and to expel, its members.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation_of_behaviour_in_the_chamber">Regulation of behaviour in the chamber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Regulation of behaviour in the chamber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There are two motions which have grown up through custom and practice and which govern questionable conduct within the House. They are brought into play by a member standing up, possibly intervening on another member, and moving the motion without notice. When the debate is getting excessively heated, it is open to a member to move "that the Standing Order on Asperity of Speech be read by the Clerk". The motion can be debated,<sup id="cite_ref-Asperity_of_speech_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asperity_of_speech-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> but if agreed by the House, the Clerk of the Parliaments will read out Standing Order 33 which provides "That all personal, sharp, or taxing speeches be forborn".<sup id="cite_ref-Standing_Order_32_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Standing_Order_32-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The Journals of the House of Lords record only four instances on which the House has ordered the Standing Order to be read since the procedure was invented in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-Asperity_instances_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asperity_instances-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For more serious problems with an individual Lord, the option is available to move "That the noble Lord be no longer heard". This motion also is debatable, and the debate which ensues has sometimes offered a chance for the member whose conduct has brought it about to come to order so that the motion can be withdrawn. If the motion is passed, its effect is to prevent the member from continuing their speech on the motion then under debate.<sup id="cite_ref-No_longer_heard_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-No_longer_heard-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Journals identify eleven occasions on which this motion has been moved since 1884; four were eventually withdrawn, one was voted down, and six were passed.<sup id="cite_ref-No_longer_heard_instances_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-No_longer_heard_instances-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leave_of_absence">Leave of absence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Leave of absence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1958, to counter criticism that some peers only appeared at major decisions in the House and thereby particular votes were swayed, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Standing_Orders_of_the_House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Standing Orders of the House of Lords (page does not exist)">Standing Orders of the House of Lords</a> were enhanced.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Peers who did not wish to attend meetings regularly or were prevented by ill health, age or further reasons, were now able to request Leave of Absence.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> During the granted time a peer is expected not to visit the House\'s meetings until either its expiration or termination, announced at least a month prior to their return.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attendance_allowance">Attendance allowance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Attendance allowance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Members of the House of Lords can, since 2010, opt to receive a \xc2\xa3300 per day attendance allowance, plus limited travel expenses. Peers can elect to receive a reduced attendance allowance of \xc2\xa3150 per day instead. Prior to 2010 peers from outside London could claim an overnight allowance of \xc2\xa3174.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-telegraph-20150729_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-20150729-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Committees">Committees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Committees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Unlike in the House of Commons, when the term committee is used to describe a stage of a bill, this committee does not take the form of a <a href="/wiki/Public_bill_committee" title="Public bill committee">public bill committee</a>, but what is described as Committee of the Whole House. It is made up of all Members of the House of Lords allowing any Member to contribute to debates if he or she chooses to do so and allows for more flexible rules of procedure. It is presided over by the Chairman of Committees.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term committee is also used to describe Grand Committee, where the same rules of procedure apply as in the main chamber, except that no divisions may take place. For this reason, business that is discussed in Grand Committee is usually uncontroversial and likely to be agreed unanimously.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Public bills may also be committed to pre-legislative committees. A pre-legislative Committee is specifically constituted for a particular bill. These committees are established in advance of the bill being laid before either the House of Lords or the House of Commons and can take evidence from the public. Such committees are rare and do not replace any of the usual stages of a bill, including committee stage.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The House of Lords also has 15 <a href="/wiki/Select_Committee_(Westminster_System)" class="mw-redirect" title="Select Committee (Westminster System)">Select Committees</a>. Typically, these are <i>sessional committees</i>, meaning that their members are appointed by the House at the beginning of each session, and continue to serve until the next parliamentary session begins. In practice, these are often permanent committees, which are re-established during every session. These committees are typically empowered to make reports to the House "from time to time", that is, whenever they wish. Other committees are <i>ad-hoc committees</i>, which are set up to investigate a specific issue. When they are set up by a motion in the House, the motion will set a deadline by which the Committee must report. After this date, the Committee will cease to exist unless it is granted an extension. One example of this is the Committee on Public Service and Demographic Change.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The House of Lords may appoint a chairman for a committee; if it does not do so, the Chairman of Committees or a Deputy Chairman of Committees may preside instead. Most of the Select Committees are also granted the power to co-opt members, such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Committee" title="European Union Committee">European Union Committee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The primary function of Select Committees is to scrutinise and investigate Government activities; to fulfil these aims, they are permitted to hold hearings and collect evidence. Bills may be referred to Select Committees, but are more often sent to the Committee of the Whole House and Grand Committees.\n</p><p>The committee system of the House of Lords also includes several Domestic Committees, which supervise or consider the House\'s procedures and administration. One of the Domestic Committees is the Committee of Selection, which is responsible for assigning members to many of the House\'s other committees.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_composition">Current composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Current composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Members of the House of Lords">Members of the House of Lords</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House-of-lords-diagram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="House-of-lords-diagram.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House-of-lords-diagram.jpg/350px-House-of-lords-diagram.jpg" width="350" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House-of-lords-diagram.jpg/525px-House-of-lords-diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House-of-lords-diagram.jpg/700px-House-of-lords-diagram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="705" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House-of-lords-diagram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>There are currently 791 sitting members of the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_composition_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords_composition-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> An additional 23 Lords are ineligible from participation, including eight peers who are constitutionally disqualified as members of the Judiciary.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_ineligible_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords_ineligible-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The House of Lords Act 1999 allocated 75 of the 92 hereditary peers to the parties based on the proportion of hereditary peers that belonged to that party in 1999:<sup id="cite_ref-standing_orders1_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-standing_orders1-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Conservative Party: 42 peers</li>\n<li>Labour Party: 2 peers</li>\n<li>Liberal Democrats: 3 peers</li>\n<li>Crossbenchers: 28 peers</li></ul>\n<p>Of the initial 42 hereditary peers elected as Conservatives, one, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Verney,_21st_Baron_Willoughby_de_Broke" class="mw-redirect" title="Leopold Verney, 21st Baron Willoughby de Broke">Lord Willoughby de Broke</a>, now sits as a member of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Independence_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Independence Party">UKIP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_composition_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords_composition-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fifteen hereditary peers are elected by the whole House, and the remaining hereditary peers are the two royal office-holders, the <a href="/wiki/Earl_Marshal" title="Earl Marshal">Earl Marshal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Great_Chamberlain" title="Lord Great Chamberlain">Lord Great Chamberlain</a>, both of whom are currently on leave of absence.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_composition_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords_composition-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A report in 2007 stated that many members of the Lords (particularly the life peers) do not attend regularly; the average daily attendance was around 408.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the number of hereditary peers is limited to 92, and that of Lords spiritual to 26, there is no maximum limit to the number of life peers who may be members of the House of Lords at any time.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_leaders_and_ministers_in_the_Lords">Government leaders and ministers in the Lords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=House_of_Lords&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Government leaders and ministers in the Lords">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaders_and_chief_whips">Leaders and chief whips</span><span Baroness Evans of Bowes Park</a>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Leader of the House of Lords and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Privy_Seal" title="Lord Privy Seal">Lord Privy Seal</a> (Conservative; Cabinet member)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Curzon,_7th_Earl_Howe" title="Frederick Curzon, 7th Earl Howe">The Earl Howe</a>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Minister of State for <a of the Yeomen of the Guard</a></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ministers">Other ministers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a> applies in the United Kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_invocation_of_Article_50_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union" title="United Kingdom invocation of Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union">invokation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_from_the_European_Union" title="Withdrawal from the European Union">Article 50</a>, the United Kingdom is <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">set to leave</a> the European Union. A <a href="/wiki/European_Union_(Withdrawal)_Act_2018" title="European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018">Great Repeal Bill</a> has been proposed to incorporate all <i>current</i> European laws into <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law of the United Kingdom">British law</a>.\n<br /><sup><small>2</small></sup> "Bailiwick-wide" legislature passed in the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Guernsey" title="States of Guernsey">States of Guernsey</a> applies not only in <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a>, but also in <a href="/wiki/Alderney" title="Alderney">Alderney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sark" title="Sark">Sark</a>, with the consent of their governments.</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox"
